  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_256fea92-c0d0-4425-8bad-2eac9daea69f'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer1026231668053832900"><div>
  <p>The mining ship El Cavador is far out from Earth, in the deeps of the Kuiper Belt, beyond Pluto. Other mining ships, and the families that live on them, are few and far between this far out. So when El Cavador's telescopes pick up a fast-moving object coming in-system, it's hard to know what to make of it. It's massive and moving at a significant fraction of the speed o</p></div></span>
  <span id="freeText1026231668053832900" style="display:none"><div>
  <p>The mining ship El Cavador is far out from Earth, in the deeps of the Kuiper Belt, beyond Pluto. Other mining ships, and the families that live on them, are few and far between this far out. So when El Cavador's telescopes pick up a fast-moving object coming in-system, it's hard to know what to make of it. It's massive and moving at a significant fraction of the speed of light.<br /><br />El Cavador has other problems. Their systems are old and failing. The family is getting too big for the ship. There are claim-jumping corporate ships bringing Asteroid Belt tactics to the Kuiper Belt. Worrying about a distant object that might or might not be an alien ship seems not important.<br /><br />They're wrong. It's the most important thing that has happened to the human race in a million years. The first Formic War is about to begin.</p>
</div></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer365299310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6145690846423371963"><b>Read <a href='http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/2012/08/review-earth-unaware-by-orson-scott.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>This Review</a> & More Like It <a href='http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>On My Blog</a>!</b><br/><br/>Written as a prequel to the well-loved <i>Ender's Game</i>, Johnston's <i>Earth Unaware</i> tries to fill in some of the holes and unexplored history of the "Enderverse" and the first Formic War that led to Battle School, and Ender's adventures in vanquishing the "hormigas"/Formics. When this book works the most, it succeeds predominately on misplaced nostalgia for the earlier-published-but-later-in-the-chronology novels like <i>Ender's Game, Xenophobe, Children of the </i></span>
  <span id="freeText6145690846423371963" style="display:none"><b>Read <a href="http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/2012/08/review-earth-unaware-by-orson-scott.html" rel="nofollow noopener">This Review</a> &amp; More Like It <a href="http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">On My Blog</a>!</b><br /><br />Written as a prequel to the well-loved <i>Ender's Game</i>, Johnston's <i>Earth Unaware</i> tries to fill in some of the holes and unexplored history of the "Enderverse" and the first Formic War that led to Battle School, and Ender's adventures in vanquishing the "hormigas"/Formics. When this book works the most, it succeeds predominately on misplaced nostalgia for the earlier-published-but-later-in-the-chronology novels like <i>Ender's Game, Xenophobe, Children of the Mind</i>, etc. I found <i>Earth Unaware</i> to be a weak, ghost-written book that lacks the easy charisma, dynamic characters, and unique storyline that the other books possessed in abundance and which made them so memorable.<br /><br />There are obviously some good, interesting ideas at play here (the asteroid mining and the cultures that sprout up around them [free miners versus corporations, etc.]) but Aaron Johnston is primarily a graphic novelist, and it shows quite obviously here. Nothing about the novel is realized to its full potential -- from characters to plot to even the action, almost all about <i>Earth Unaware</i> felt contrived, weak, and overdone all at the same time. This is a superficial and shallow adaptation of Ender and the world's backstory, obviously written primarily to lure in fans of <i>Ender's Game</i> and its subsequent sequels. The plot is minimal, the characters are in dire need of more/or a rounded personality (or in Wit's case, a connection to the actual story. His Earth-bound plot will surely coincide with the events of the sequels, but for <i>Earth Unaware</i>, they are more filler than anything else, Mazer Rackham cameo or not.)<br /><br />Wonky pacing, uneven and unconnected storylines, cliched or predictable characters, and more made this a miss for me. The few things I found interesting were often and quickly glossed over to focus on the less developed ideas and characters. There are people who will absolutely love this and gush over the finally explained and explored first contact with the Formics, but <i>Earth Unaware</i> is nowhere near the league of <i>Ender's Game</i> in any area. This review is much shorter than most, but my disappointment with this and OSC's raging homophobia make it almost impossible for an impartial thought.<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>And other thoughts:</b>
</blockquote><br /><br />When I first read <i>Ender's Game</i>, I was 10. It was my first scifi novel and Ender was a protagonist seemingly created just for me to love. I still love it to this day, but more for nostalgia and my first sense of how powerful children could be than for anything else. It was revelatory: kids can be heroes and save the world too! Now that I'm older, wiser, and more exposed to the kind of hate that OSC regularly spews towards homosexuals, I find myself less and less inclined to pick up anything he's written (or was written for him.) I debated whether or not to even review this (though it's far from a glowing review) because I don't want to promote OSC in any way, shape or form, negatively or not. In this recent climate, among all these debates about author behavior and how it affects readers, I find it hard to justify my read of this/these books. Sure, OSC has never attacked a negative review or reviewer (to my knowledge, but I certainly try to ignore anything that comes out of his mouth at this point), but how authors behave does impact their work and those who read it. <br /><br />As I was reading <i>Speechless</i> by Hannah Harrington right after this novel, it made me think about silent compliance, ignoring the bad stuff, and just doing what everyone else does for the sake of not making waves. I'm done, I'm gonna make my own wave about this; I just can't support an author who thinks it's right to discriminate against and dehumanize other people. I was granted an ARC of this, but you can bet this author will never see another penny of my cash. I won't be finishing the First Formic War series, and though I thank TOR for the generosity of reading the ARC, even an ARC of the sequel won't tempt me. Goodbye, OSC. I will still reread <i>Ender</i>, but I won't recommend it anyone anymore.<br /><br />So long, Enderverse, and thanks for all the fish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6145690846423371963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15917055937571049297">As a huge fan of the Enderverse, I knew that I would like Earth Unaware. I was not aware (hah!) of how *much* I would like it- I found it gripping and nearly impossible to put down.<br/><br/>Like Michael Flynn's "Wreck of the River of Stars", there is no question how the Formica wars turn out: very, very badly for the humans. We know that from the basic premise of the universe. So knowing the doom that is coming could lend a funereal tone to the work, but in Card and Johnston's hands it does not. The char</span>
  <span id="freeText15917055937571049297" style="display:none">As a huge fan of the Enderverse, I knew that I would like Earth Unaware. I was not aware (hah!) of how *much* I would like it- I found it gripping and nearly impossible to put down.<br /><br />Like Michael Flynn's "Wreck of the River of Stars", there is no question how the Formica wars turn out: very, very badly for the humans. We know that from the basic premise of the universe. So knowing the doom that is coming could lend a funereal tone to the work, but in Card and Johnston's hands it does not. The characters are compelling, and of course we have no idea *which* people will live through these doomed battles, but their motivations are clear and relatable.<br /><br />One extremely good thing about the book is that there is no reason at all that one need have read "Ender's Game" first: this does stand on its own, and while there are a few references to things and people who will become significant, the references are not the crucial part- all of them have perfectly valid in-story reasons for being there.<br /><br />Excellent, and highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_428280814" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/428280814" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/763559-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277400707p2/763559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/428280814?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/763559-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer428280814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9618158537208737388">I didn't have high expectations going into <i>Earth Unaware</i>. It's a collaboration, and even though Orson Scott Card's name is plastered on the front it's a good bet that Aaron Johnston did most of the writing. I wasn't particularly fond of their last collaboration, <i>Invasive Procedures</i>, where it felt (to me) like someone else writing a book based on an Orson Scott Card idea. For that matter, I haven't been overwhelmed by the recent Enderverse books such as <i>Shadows in Flight</i> and <i>Ender in Exile</i>.<br/><br/>Well, </span>
  <span id="freeText9618158537208737388" style="display:none">I didn't have high expectations going into <i>Earth Unaware</i>. It's a collaboration, and even though Orson Scott Card's name is plastered on the front it's a good bet that Aaron Johnston did most of the writing. I wasn't particularly fond of their last collaboration, <i>Invasive Procedures</i>, where it felt (to me) like someone else writing a book based on an Orson Scott Card idea. For that matter, I haven't been overwhelmed by the recent Enderverse books such as <i>Shadows in Flight</i> and <i>Ender in Exile</i>.<br /><br />Well, I'm pleased to say that <i>Earth Unaware</i> <i>does</i> feel like an Orson Scott Card book. But more importantly, it's a really <i>good</i> story. As a prequel, it begins to explain some of the technology and backstory that leads into <i>Ender's Game</i>, but it isn't a slave to those things. There's a whole new cast of characters to love (or love to hate!) and we're immersed in the frontier-like setting of the Kuiper belt on the outer rim of the solar system, where mining families scrape a living by harvesting metal from asteroids. Even before the Buggers (sorry, Formics) show up, there's plenty of conflict and difficult choices to make. And once they realize an alien ship is heading towards Earth, everything in their already complicated life is thrown into chaos. In other words, this isn't just filling a gap in the Enderverse backstory; this is good sci fi and a story that can stand on its own legs.<br /><br />Well, it stands on its own relative to <i>Ender's Game</i> and the subsequent books. As far as the story of the Formic Wars, this is just the beginning. There is a bit of resolution, but <i>Earth Unaware</i> ends with the promise of all the biggest events still to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9618158537208737388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_431489852" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/431489852" />
    <a title="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions"><img alt="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464118116p2/2602702.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/431489852?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina (A Reader of Fictions)" href="/user/show/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions">Christina (A Reader of Fictions)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781427221544" href="/book/show/13167840-earth-unaware">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2602702-christina-a-reader-of-fictions?shelf=series-not-finishing">series-not-finishing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer431489852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13426841934104238181">I've read Ender's Game two or three times, and I love it. Needless to say, the fact that it was getting a prequel series filled me with curiosity and trepidation in about equal measures. While finding out about first contact with the aliens could be interesting, Card could definitely much it all up with his asshole opinions. Turns out, though, that this was mostly neither. Card didn't assault me with a religious message, but this book also just was not that good.<br/><br/>The first chapters of Earth Unawa</span>
  <span id="freeText13426841934104238181" style="display:none">I've read Ender's Game two or three times, and I love it. Needless to say, the fact that it was getting a prequel series filled me with curiosity and trepidation in about equal measures. While finding out about first contact with the aliens could be interesting, Card could definitely much it all up with his asshole opinions. Turns out, though, that this was mostly neither. Card didn't assault me with a religious message, but this book also just was not that good.<br /><br />The first chapters of Earth Unaware are simply interminable. I cannot for the life of me fathom why Card and Johnston thought that it would be an awesome idea to start the book the way they did. Up until the time the aliens make an appearance and the action scenes begin, this book was incredibly boring.<br /><br />The entire novel is told in alternating third person limited. Most of the time, the narration follows three main characters: Victor, Lem, and Wit. However, interspersed between these are brief sections from the points of view of other characters. These I found a bit obnoxious, particularly when the novel lost the nice orderly method of starting a new chapter for each new perspective. Also annoying is the fact that Wit's storyline never really syncs up with the others, though I do know why he's included.<br /><br />Alright, now that you have the gist of it, you need to hear about the first chapter. We begin with Victor, a teenager and accomplished mechanic.When we meet him, he is in the midst of all of the angst. He and his second cousin, Alejandra, have been accused of inappropriate feelings for one another, much too close for cousins. Because of their improper closeness, Alejandra is being zogged, married off to someone on another mining ship. <br /><br />Victor at first whines about the unfairness of this accusation, pissed off that no one understands that they are but friends. Then, he thinks of a time when Alejandra gave him 'a look,' and decides that she did have feelings for him. As he continues to ponder this, he decides that he too loves her, and that she has been sent away for the best, and that he too must leave El Cavador, their family's mining vessel, sometime soon because he cannot get over her surrounded by memories of her. Also, I feel like they must be trying to make some sort of statement, since, otherwise, the same feeling could have been established without their being related.<br /><br />That is the entire first chapter. Honestly, I have NO clue whether Card and Johnston want people to root for the two of them or to be disgusted at the thought of second cousins in love or what. Worse still, it became apparent that this incredibly unpleasant plot device had been put in place solely to up the melodrama of the novel. This gives everyone a reason to mope about and be sad and do stupid things in an attempt to find her. All they do with her character is kill her off, without the reader ever meeting her. Later in the book, Victor even realizes that he didn't love her like that after all, a revelation that incensed me even more after having had to listen to so much of his weepy angst over their separation.<br /><br />Another thing that bothered me about this opening and the novel in general was the sexism. When charges were brought against the two, Alejandra was sent away from her home to be married off hastily as punishment, and Victor had no change in status, except for extra sympathy from some and anger from Alejandra's father. Really? Apparently, future humans do not believe in the strength of women at all, having regressed from the current climate. With the notable exception of El Cavador's captain and a teenage girl who works the eye (which watches for threats to the ship), the women all stick to traditional female roles, like parenting. None get to help defend the ship. Two strong female characters do not make up for suggesting that in the future most women will be forced back into a powerless role. Also, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[both of the strong female characters, along with all the men, die because of the emotional decisions of the female captain. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Lem, too, is annoying; all of his sections consist of his bitching and moaning about his daddy issues. *yawns* Wit was my favorite perspective, but it felt like Card and Johnston continually forgot he was there. Much was made in his introduction of the recruitment of Mazer Rackham, a name I recognize from Ender's Game, though I do not remember the significance, but nothing else is made of him for the rest of the book, which is incredibly sloppy.<br /><br />If, however, you feel compelled to read everything set in the Enderverse, then I recommend audio over print, because I definitely think I would have had to DNF the physical book. <br /><br />Narration:<br />Macmillan Audio procured an almost full cast for this production, using a different voice actor for the different third person perspectives. Most of them do a pretty good job, though I really think they could have chosen better voice actors in several cases and done a better job with accents. The worst casting error in my opinion was Victor. He's supposed to be a teenager, but the voice actor sounds much older. The crew of El Cavador is hispanic, but only some of the voice actors used an accent when reading. In the cast of accents, it should really be all or nothing.<br /><br />The best narration was done by the guy who voiced Wit. He has this incredibly deep voice that perfectly matched the gruff soldier. However, he also was a whiz with accents and could change the depth of his voice to match the different characters. It seems as though the producers knew this guy was the best, because he, for some reason, voiced for two characters' perspectives, while everyone else just voiced one. Sure, one of them only had a very short section, but, still, couldn't they hire someone for that?<br /><br /><br />Find more of my reviews, and other fun stuff, at <a href="http://readeroffictions.blogspot.com" rel="nofollow noopener">A Reader of Fictions</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13426841934104238181" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_426315107" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/426315107" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11220280-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343073879p2/11220280.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

  <span id="freeText15345607242250611894" style="display:none">I didn't know this book existed until a coworker dropped it on my desk. I might have been happier then. Super wooden dialogue, weird conceits (15 and 9 year old boys would be inventing amazing things, if only the fascist system didn't make them go to school!) There's a healthy heap of Mormon social roles/values, which I realize I am somewhat sensitized to, but didn't endear the book to me any further.<br /><br />The worst part was that this didn't actually tell the whole prequel; it just started. Ugh. I doubt I'll be picking up any more.<br /><br />(I found out from the end-notes that this novel was adapted from a comic book, which does make a little bit of sense with the weird structure/feel of the book.)</span>

<div id="review_803901400" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/803901400" />
    <a title="Dave" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1235578-dave"><img alt="Dave" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/803901400?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave" href="/user/show/1235578-dave">Dave</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer803901400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5544605075510082306">I enjoyed the story, but despised the poor basic physics in this book. That has made it me least favorite of Card's books. Add the fact that it is an unresolved ending (unlike any of his other 12 or so books I've read) and it's 2 stars for me. <br/><br/>For those interested, the physics blunders mostly focused on a misunderstanding of relative velocity and what's speed means in space. They make a big deal out of a high speed docking maneuver in space, but that is absurd. As long as the 2 ships match each </span>
  <span id="freeText5544605075510082306" style="display:none">I enjoyed the story, but despised the poor basic physics in this book. That has made it me least favorite of Card's books. Add the fact that it is an unresolved ending (unlike any of his other 12 or so books I've read) and it's 2 stars for me. <br /><br />For those interested, the physics blunders mostly focused on a misunderstanding of relative velocity and what's speed means in space. They make a big deal out of a high speed docking maneuver in space, but that is absurd. As long as the 2 ships match each other in speed, the have a relative speed of 0. They don't need to keep engines running to maintain their speed because there is no air resistance and no friction. They also talk about only being able to make repairs at a full stop, but there is no such thing as stopped in space! Stopped compared to what? The earth is moving at one speed through the solar system while everything else moves a different speed, except the sun, which is also moving compared to everything else in the galaxy. Finally, they talk about space dust as though space dust is all moving in similar ways at similar speeds and of significant amounts in between different planets and asteroid belts. It is likely to be so sparse as to not matter, and is going to be moving in random directions at random speeds based on the exploding and mining they talk about in the book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5544605075510082306" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_729382327" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/729382327" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/580529-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217002299p2/580529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/729382327?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/580529-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer729382327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7557333912288832589">This book was not good. There was nothing grievously wrong with it - except the author's apparent complete lack of understanding of physics - but there were lots of little things that just rankled. First of all, some of the audiobook narrators were terrible, particularly the ones who voiced Victor and Lem. If you're going to write about a Venezuelan family of space miners you'd better find somebody who can pronounce Spanish words properly.<br/><br/>None of the characters were that great and many of them f</span>
  <span id="freeText7557333912288832589" style="display:none">This book was not good. There was nothing grievously wrong with it - except the author's apparent complete lack of understanding of physics - but there were lots of little things that just rankled. First of all, some of the audiobook narrators were terrible, particularly the ones who voiced Victor and Lem. If you're going to write about a Venezuelan family of space miners you'd better find somebody who can pronounce Spanish words properly.<br /><br />None of the characters were that great and many of them felt gimmicky. As an example, we first meet Victor as he's learning that his childhood friend is being sent away so he doesn't fall in love with her - a big no-no in space mining families. His internal monologue as he comes to the realization that he actually <i>does</i> love her is everything that is wrong about young adult novels. Then he spends the rest of the book pining for this character that we never meet. I guess it's supposed to make him relatable but it really just makes me want to deck him. A lot of the other characters are just way too <i>pure</i>. There's practically no depth to any of them.<br /><br />And the physics. Oh the physics. One thing you learn pretty quickly in space is that velocity doesn't really mean a whole lot. Acceleration means something and <i>relative</i> velocity it kind of important but the idea that you can't do something in space because you're going too fast is ludicrous. Too fast in relation to what? The air outside? Because there isn't any. Just a whole lot of nothing. The danger of space dust was brought up as a reason to slow down before a space walk but that assumes that the space dust is all holding still, which is preposterous. It's whizzing all over the place in every direction regardless of how fast you happen to be going. The prime example of this idiocy was the super dangerous "high speed" docking operation that takes place towards the end of the book. It's dangerous for ships to dock at high speeds? Why? As long as their <i>relative</i> velocities are similar it is as if they are holding still, because for all intents and purposes, they are. If this only came up a time or two, it would have been irritating but maybe I could have overlooked it. Unfortunately, it came up over and over and over again and was a serious factor in my enjoyment of the story.<br /><br />I really expected better from Card.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7557333912288832589" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_388789373" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388789373" />
    <a title="Rich Kulesus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6156575-rich-kulesus"><img alt="Rich Kulesus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314633072p2/6156575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388789373?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rich Kulesus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rich Kulesus" href="/user/show/6156575-rich-kulesus">Rich Kulesus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Die-hard Ender's Game Fanatics</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388789373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1859749822905313982"><b>Earth Unaware is to Orson Scott Card what "Lady in the Water" was to M. Night Shyamalan</b> - The latest disappointing tale in a descending career abruptly punctuated by explanatory paragraphs to beat you over the head with every concept since he couldn't work it in more craftily. Ender's Game was Card's brilliant supernova of a novel, which, Like Shyamalan's Unbreakable and The Sixth Sense, expertly twirled the reader's mind an incredible twist ending that left one pondering the meaning for days to</span>
  <span id="freeText1859749822905313982" style="display:none"><b>Earth Unaware is to Orson Scott Card what "Lady in the Water" was to M. Night Shyamalan</b> - The latest disappointing tale in a descending career abruptly punctuated by explanatory paragraphs to beat you over the head with every concept since he couldn't work it in more craftily. Ender's Game was Card's brilliant supernova of a novel, which, Like Shyamalan's Unbreakable and The Sixth Sense, expertly twirled the reader's mind an incredible twist ending that left one pondering the meaning for days to come. Unfortunately both supernova's have faded with time, descending to ham-fisted story-telling.<br /><br />You're not smart enough to figure things out on your own, or so Card believes. If you choose to read this (and I don't recommend you do), you'll be confronted by a silly vocabulary of items and concepts that are spelled out in parenthetical explanatory paragraphs to ensure you clearly, unequivocally, and resolutely understand what greaves, quickships, zogging, and dogging are. You're not smart enough to figure it out, and must have it explained to you, like a child.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the author himself might not have it figured out. The Kuiper belt ecosystem and shockingly poor understanding of physics (plasma, ramscoops, and radio interference) will cause even moderate fans of sci-fi to cry foul. The failure to impress becomes clearer when reading the novel's Afterword - this work is preparation for a comic book audience.<br /><br />Need I say more? Writing a book to prepare for a comic series - priceless.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1859749822905313982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/382547235" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6789405-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/382547235?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/6789405-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/15746760-earth-unaware">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer382547235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4099689667925262587">Ender's Game is still one of my top 5 books of all time. While I didn't like the followup novels as much -- they didn't quite my satiate my reading tastes as much as Ender's Game -- I could still appreciate how well they were written. Earth Unaware, however, is a completely different beast. Seeing that the book was co-authored should have set alarm bells ringing in my head, but I was excited to read a new Card novel, so... <br/><br/>Earth Unaware is more of a series of short stories that are loosely tied </span>
  <span id="freeText4099689667925262587" style="display:none">Ender's Game is still one of my top 5 books of all time. While I didn't like the followup novels as much -- they didn't quite my satiate my reading tastes as much as Ender's Game -- I could still appreciate how well they were written. Earth Unaware, however, is a completely different beast. Seeing that the book was co-authored should have set alarm bells ringing in my head, but I was excited to read a new Card novel, so... <br /><br />Earth Unaware is more of a series of short stories that are loosely tied together rather than a novel. It also includes couple of utterly pointless cameo appearances by characters which will play a role later in the Formic wars, but which have little impact at all in this book. <br /><br />This utterly un-Card-like production starts to make sense after reading the Afterward in the book -- it was never originally intended to be a novel. It was backstory that Card created for Ender's Game. Back in 2009 Marvel Comics made several successful runs of comics based on Ender's Game and the Ender universe. This book is the result of Marvel wanting to do a new comic book series with new characters, but still set in the Ender universe. It was created specifically to flesh out the story for the comic books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4099689667925262587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_372280545" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/372280545" />
    <a title="Scott Danielson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2867426-scott-danielson"><img alt="Scott Danielson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392053958p2/2867426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/372280545?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Danielson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Danielson" href="/user/show/2867426-scott-danielson">Scott Danielson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2867426-scott-danielson?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2867426-scott-danielson?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer372280545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1050639945631642642">One of the pleasures of listening to science fiction audiobooks over the years has been hearing Orson Scott Card's <i>Ender</i> series. Besides being expertly narrated by an ensemble led by Stefan Rudnicki, these audiobooks are entertaining because Card isn't delivering the same book over and over. In <b>Earth Unaware</b>, Orson Scott Card and Aaron Johnston take the series in yet another direction.<br/><br/>I know, I know. It's been proven time after time. When a book series gets to the point where <i>[Original Author]</i> p</span>
  <span id="freeText1050639945631642642" style="display:none">One of the pleasures of listening to science fiction audiobooks over the years has been hearing Orson Scott Card's <i>Ender</i> series. Besides being expertly narrated by an ensemble led by Stefan Rudnicki, these audiobooks are entertaining because Card isn't delivering the same book over and over. In <b>Earth Unaware</b>, Orson Scott Card and Aaron Johnston take the series in yet another direction.<br /><br />I know, I know. It's been proven time after time. When a book series gets to the point where <i>[Original Author]</i> picks up <i>[Insert new author here (often a relative)]</i>, the results are just... not good. I'm happy to report that <i>Earth Unaware</i> is an excellent novel. I thoroughly enjoyed it.<br /><br />Aaron Johnston and Orson Scott Card created and are telling the story of the First Formic War in the comic format. I haven't read those, so I can't say how similar this novel is, but Aaron Johnston says in the Afterword that <b>Earth Unaware</b> draws from the characters and events in those comics.<br /><br />The subtitle (First Formic War) implies that we're in for a military SF novel, but that's not what this is. This novel is a tense near-space adventure set in the not too distant future and peopled with characters I cared about. The opening reveals the thoughts and feelings of teenager on the <i>El Calvador</i>, a mining ship in the Kuiper Belt. Close by, on a different ship, is a man who has invested much time and effort into the invention of a gravity laser. He needs to prove his worth to his corporate employer. And back on Earth, an elite military unit is being formed. These lives, some entwined, move forward as normal until all interests are altered in the face of the arrival of an alien ship in the solar system.<br /><br />Even though the cover doesn't say it, this is Book 1 of at least a few. I look forward to the continued development of the concept of difference. On Valentine Wiggin's Hierarchy of Foreignness is <i>Varelse</i>. True aliens, aliens so alien that we can't even communicate with them or even hope to understand them. How could war with such a race be avoided? Difference also extends to human beings, who seem so content to drop their conflicts in the face of greater danger. Why is that what it takes?<br /><br />The audiobook is performed by multiple narrators in the style that fits Orson Scott Card's stories so incredibly well. The narrators (all excellent) change with the POV of the story. Reading the story were: Stefan Rudnicki, Stephen Hoye, Arthur Morey, Vikas Adam, Emily Janice Card, Gabrielle de Cuir, and Roxanne Hernandez. Top notch!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1050639945631642642" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_776857545" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/776857545" />
    <a title="John Sorensen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6024071-john-sorensen"><img alt="John Sorensen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/776857545?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Sorensen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Sorensen" href="/user/show/6024071-john-sorensen">John Sorensen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6024071-john-sorensen?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer776857545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4500175403265331761">The story was typical Orson Scott Card. Solid characters, I wanted to read more. I was entertained. However, the physics and common-sense errors in this book were absolutely horrible, it made me want to scream. If that was it, I would give the book 4 stars.<br/><br/>Here is an example: In the first chapters of the book, the need for one of the characters to give an update to the board, drove all of the action - to speed up tests. Then that same character, who had to do a bunch of things for the updates to</span>
  <span id="freeText4500175403265331761" style="display:none">The story was typical Orson Scott Card. Solid characters, I wanted to read more. I was entertained. However, the physics and common-sense errors in this book were absolutely horrible, it made me want to scream. If that was it, I would give the book 4 stars.<br /><br />Here is an example: In the first chapters of the book, the need for one of the characters to give an update to the board, drove all of the action - to speed up tests. Then that same character, who had to do a bunch of things for the updates to the board, then gave no updates at all for about a year. Just not consistent, not believable. Then the physics were horrible. For example - to do repairs, the ship in deep space had to "stop". Stop relative to what? (Yeah, um it's called the theory of relativity Card &amp; Johnston) Currently, when they need to do repairs on the International Space Station (ISS) - they don't stop the space station. They just do the repairs - its called a space walk. There were about a dozen, obvious, egregious assaults on common sense, and the laws of physics. There is no excuse for this in modern science fiction. (-1 star for just being stupid and awful)<br /><br />The best part of Card's writing is when he discusses the deep motives of the characters and reveals their thinking. Not present. (-1 star), and thus my 2 star rating.<br /><br />However, I did want to finish reading the series, and I have read the second book, but this is a low point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4500175403265331761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17014624662710580908"> A decent enough entry in the Ender saga. I would have preferred that all the various characters and plot points got tied together more before ending the book. All the MOPs backstory was a waste of time, briefly introducing Mazer Rackham and then booting him offstage. We know we'll see him again, but in this first book, it seems very gratuitous.   <span id="freeText17014624662710580908" style="display:none"> A decent enough entry in the Ender saga. I would have preferred that all the various characters and plot points got tied together more before ending the book. All the MOPs backstory was a waste of time, briefly introducing Mazer Rackham and then booting him offstage. We know we'll see him again, but in this first book, it seems very gratuitous. Most of the science of this book is very fictional, so check your science background at the door, if you've got one. Finding out that the book was written to flesh out the storyline in a bunch of comics might explain some of the awkwardness of the plot. Ultimately, it's fun enough to explore the Ender backstory that I'll be continuing this series, despite these flaws.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3106707110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15076989244197840952">The story took a bit to get into, but that's not offputting to me because world- and character-building in speculative fiction often requires that, especially those with an epic style. This story was pretty fascinating and kept our interest rapt on a very long road trip. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_391260067" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/391260067" />
    <a title="Eric Herboso" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1363493-eric-herboso"><img alt="Eric Herboso" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327634534p2/1363493.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/391260067?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric Herboso" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric Herboso" href="/user/show/1363493-eric-herboso">Eric Herboso</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1363493-eric-herboso?shelf=soft-science-fiction">soft-science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer391260067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer397653124216819792">I went into this thinking I'd be reading the story of the elusive Mazer Rackham. Without giving anything away, let me say that this is <em>much</em> more than that.<br/><br/>As the first in a planned trilogy, I am a little upset that I can't yet read the sequels which have yet to come out, but this is actually a very good sign when it comes to how good a novel is. I'm extremely pumped by the story, even though (since it's a prequel) I technically know what's going to happen.<br/><br/>Note that there are some marvel comics a</span>
  <span id="freeText397653124216819792" style="display:none">I went into this thinking I'd be reading the story of the elusive Mazer Rackham. Without giving anything away, let me say that this is <em>much</em> more than that.<br /><br />As the first in a planned trilogy, I am a little upset that I can't yet read the sequels which have yet to come out, but this is actually a very good sign when it comes to how good a novel is. I'm extremely pumped by the story, even though (since it's a prequel) I technically know what's going to happen.<br /><br />Note that there are some marvel comics associated with this book, but they are completely unnecessary, even as a supplement. If you plan to read both, read the book first. (This is true even though the comics were published earlier than the book.)<br /><br />However, despite the five star rating, I have (as usual) some gripes about Orson Scott Card's writing.<br /><br />Please stop reading this review if you have yet to read the novel.<br /><br />First, I get incest is bad when it comes to reproduction. But geez. He is so harsh in his world building here. Sure, it's justified somewhat by the fact that there would have to be <em>some</em> system in place to deal with space-faring communities that lived out their lives in the kuiper belt. But unfortunately, Card has lost the ability to make such justifications up with me. In other books of the Ender series, he has bashed homosexuality (giving aiuia bonding as in-universe justification), islam (by making it so muslims in Ender's universe are basically evil in principle), and polyamory (in insisting upon monogamous heterosexual marriage EVEN when there literally is not enough men to go around on a colony) -- so when he bashes incest here, I just assume it is Card being his usual dick self again rather than accepting the justification given in the novel itself.<br /><br />Still, it's better justified than his other crazy notions, so I'd almost forgiven him for it. But then he drops a bombshell.<br /><br />Card makes a scientist character say that gravity is the strongest force there is.<br /><br />This reeks of utter did-not-do-the-research. Gravity is the <em>weakest</em> force we know of, not the strongest. To have a scientist who specializes in gravity say this is the pinnacle of absurdity.<br /><br />Look, I get that this is not hard science fiction. But the soft scifi label is what allows Card to come up with stuff like glasers, which I accept just as readily as I do tar trek's transporters. I've got no problem with glasers in my fiction. But please, for the love of science, do not make a scientist character say something so utterly nonscientific like this. It really takes me out of the story. /c:<br /><br />Anyway, thankfully these drawbacks do not detract too much from the rest of the story. Any fan of the Ender universe will no doubt be enthralled by this new entrant in the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="397653124216819792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1654891622369114365" style="display:none">While this book says it's by Orson Scott Card, I'm wondering if most of the writing was actually done by Aaron Johnston, who worked w/ Card while writing the comic book version of this story. <br /><br />If you'll remember, I accidentally read book 2 of this series first. <div id="review_1830999251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1830999251" />
    <a title="thethousanderclub" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20880982-thethousanderclub"><img alt="thethousanderclub" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524015087p2/20880982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1830999251?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="thethousanderclub" class="user" itemprop="url" name="thethousanderclub" href="/user/show/20880982-thethousanderclub">thethousanderclub</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1830999251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11072316502034004292">I now have read twelve books in the Ender Universe. And, sadly, I think I'm done reading books in that universe. The last book I read from Scott Card was Ender in Exile, and I largely left off reading that book with a positive feeling. I wrote in part: "With so many books and so many authors in the wild to enjoy, I'm not exactly sure why I keep coming back to the Ender well. Regardless of whether I figure it out or not, I'll be back to take another drink and more than likely enjoy the taste just</span>
  <span id="freeText11072316502034004292" style="display:none">I now have read twelve books in the Ender Universe. And, sadly, I think I'm done reading books in that universe. The last book I read from Scott Card was Ender in Exile, and I largely left off reading that book with a positive feeling. I wrote in part: "With so many books and so many authors in the wild to enjoy, I'm not exactly sure why I keep coming back to the Ender well. Regardless of whether I figure it out or not, I'll be back to take another drink and more than likely enjoy the taste just fine." Yet, going into Earth Unaware my mood and feeling changed. I realized that with so many books to read and so many authors to enjoy, it may be time to leave behind characters I have come to love.<br /><br />The most interesting aspect of Earth Unaware is the new cast of characters. As I have read about Ender and the characters that surround him, such as his family, I have come to know them in an intimate way, even personal. Furthermore, with Scott Card's signature psycho-analysis, the reader came to know the characters at a very deep, albeit sometimes trite, way. Earth Unaware only tries to bridge the current story with the future story by briefly introducing but just as quickly leaving behind the war hero Mazer Rackham. I was fine with the introduction but also the quick departure from him. We have learned enough about the characters from the original Ender stories. It was time for new blood, new motivations, and new conflicts. The new characters are adequate but mostly forgettable. Furthermore, the story that surrounds them is also forgettable; therefore, as you can imagine, a forgettable story and forgettable characters makes for a forgettable book.<br /><br />Prequels often seem like a good idea on paper; yet, they quickly become bad ideas in their execution. Ender's Game is rightfully considered a classic of science fiction. Logic would suggest that the story that led to Ender's Game would be just as interesting. In this case, as in the case with many other prequels, it's just not true. Sometimes there is great value in mystery. When it comes to fiction, we don't have to know everything. In Ender's Game the characters, including Andrew Wiggin, and the reader are given only glimpses into the First Formic War. Wouldn't it be fascinating to get the detailed story? Not really. Storytellers should remember the lesson of the Star Wars prequels. Do we really want to know how Darth Vader became Darth Vader? It seems like a no-brainer, but the end result is pretty lousy. <br /><br />It's entirely possible I'll end up reading some more books in the Enderverse. It won't be for some time. I have no desire to continue the Earth trilogy nor the Shadow series; therefore, I don't really have too many places to go. Yet, with twelve books in my collection, I would say I put in my time as a faithful fan. At this point, I think I'm okay with remembering the great stories Scott Card gave me and forgetting the mediocre ones. Earth Unaware is the latter.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://thethousanderclub.blogspot.com/">http://thethousanderclub.blogspot.com/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11072316502034004292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_741549010" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/741549010" />
    <a title="Robert Blaylock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19612778-robert-blaylock"><img alt="Robert Blaylock" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/741549010?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Blaylock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Blaylock" href="/user/show/19612778-robert-blaylock">Robert Blaylock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer741549010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12509861572839991392">I won volume two of this series in a drawing on Goodreads. Not wanting to read the series out of sequence I bought EARTH UNAWARE in paperback. While the book successfully (and, I hope, intentionally) mimics the tone of ENDER'S GAME, I suspect that much of the writing here is actually by Aaron Johnston. That's not a bad thing. In fact, despite some readers' qualms about gaffes in the the physics utilized in the book, I found most of the scientific exposition to be considerably more detailed than </span>
  <span id="freeText12509861572839991392" style="display:none">I won volume two of this series in a drawing on Goodreads. Not wanting to read the series out of sequence I bought EARTH UNAWARE in paperback. While the book successfully (and, I hope, intentionally) mimics the tone of ENDER'S GAME, I suspect that much of the writing here is actually by Aaron Johnston. That's not a bad thing. In fact, despite some readers' qualms about gaffes in the the physics utilized in the book, I found most of the scientific exposition to be considerably more detailed than in most of Card's work that I've read. That said, I don't read science fiction for scientific accuracy; I read it first for entertainment and secondly for the speculative aspect - the "what if ...?". Keeping in mind that the story happens in the not terribly distant future, I would expect that some of what we think we know now may well have been rocked by the time the events of this book unfold. In the real world, the last one hundred and fifty years have seen several rewrites of our science texts as new discoveries and inventions have changed our understanding of what is true.<br /><br />Now that that's out of the way, on to the story. Or more accurately, my impressions of the story. While I suspect that much of the actual writing is Johnston's, the plot is obviously an expansion of backstory that Card had created for ENDER'S GAME years ago. Victor Delgado is a likeable, sympathetic character who, like many of Card's best characters, rises to the occasion to do something that would be perceived to be beyond his limitations. Lem Jukes, on the other hand, although his character moves away from being completely self-absorbed as he is when we first meet him, never quite becomes likeable. In fact, by the end of this volume, I almost felt sorry for the guy. Thinking that you're large and in charge, only to find that you're being controlled, is never pretty.<br /><br />I don't want to give too many spoilers that may discourage someone from reading the book, so just let me say here that I enjoyed the book enough that, as soon as I finished EARTH UNAWARE, I immediately picked up my copy of EARTH AFIRE to read next. While it's not in the same league with ENDER'S GAME, it serves its purpose well as a prequel. All in all, a good read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12509861572839991392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_850348916" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/850348916" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1353392-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521752891p2/1353392.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/850348916?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/1353392-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer850348916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12012315531566175005">After Card and Aaron Johnston began writing the Ender's Game comic book series, they began to see that what happened prior to Ender's story deserved a story of its own. Hence, Earth Unaware, having to do with the events leading up to the first Formic War. <br/>Vico is young, but a master mechanic. He and his family have a small ship, El Cavador, and they mine asteroids out in the Kuiper Belt. They are the first to discover an anomalous ship heading their way. Lem Jukes, heir to Jukes Unlimited, a rut</span>
  <span id="freeText12012315531566175005" style="display:none">After Card and Aaron Johnston began writing the Ender's Game comic book series, they began to see that what happened prior to Ender's story deserved a story of its own. Hence, Earth Unaware, having to do with the events leading up to the first Formic War. <br />Vico is young, but a master mechanic. He and his family have a small ship, El Cavador, and they mine asteroids out in the Kuiper Belt. They are the first to discover an anomalous ship heading their way. Lem Jukes, heir to Jukes Unlimited, a ruthless corporate mining operation, is also in the area testing a new technology that uses gravity to explode asteroids so the ores inside can be collected instead of traditional mining, which involves a lot of costly and cumbersome digging with drills. Captain Wit O'Toole is on earth, putting together an elite fighting squad culled from the best of the best of the world's armies and designed to respond to "skirmishes" the governments and mainstream military can't handle. One soldier he becomes interested in is Mazer Rackham, whose name Ender's Game readers will recognize. The action skips back and forth between the two vessels and Wit's team on Earth. It's pretty slow going in the beginning, and I lost interest several times. This just didn't really grab me like a lot of Card's other work, such as Ender's Game and Seventh Son. Once the Formics were met and the action heated up, I became more invested, though. The book ended rather abruptly, I thought, so I assume there will be sequels. Sadly, knowing Card, we'll probably have to wait a few years for the next one.<br />--edited for typos</span>

<div id="review_675363039" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/675363039" />
    <a title="Jacob Ediger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22463253-jacob-ediger"><img alt="Jacob Ediger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374608975p2/22463253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/675363039?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Ediger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Ediger" href="/user/show/22463253-jacob-ediger">Jacob Ediger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer675363039" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3983339197687934599">Ender's Game is a book about people who happen to live in the future and what technology there is there to drive the story and is not explained in detail, it is left at the level of "magic." In Earth Unaware technology is used to drive the story and explained in detail. This would be OK if the author had any appreciable understanding of the technicalities of space travel, unfortunately they do not. Spaceships are treated as cars on a highway, occasionally being required to pull over to make repa</span>
  <span id="freeText3983339197687934599" style="display:none">Ender's Game is a book about people who happen to live in the future and what technology there is there to drive the story and is not explained in detail, it is left at the level of "magic." In Earth Unaware technology is used to drive the story and explained in detail. This would be OK if the author had any appreciable understanding of the technicalities of space travel, unfortunately they do not. Spaceships are treated as cars on a highway, occasionally being required to pull over to make repairs. <br /><br />Sometimes a technology in science fiction is stated to exist and the story can depend on this, if it is self consistent, even if it is impossible within the current understanding of the world, this is OK. Other times counterfactual details may be tolerated if it permits the story to explore some place or idea which would otherwise be inaccessible, this is also OK. In this story a counterfactual universe is created and maintained unnecessarily either for the author's convenience or through their ignorance and the story is less intelligent for it.<br /><br />I have not yet read the second book of the trilogy however I don't imagine much would be lost by skipping straight to the second book. At the end of this book the invaders have not even reached the inner system, almost nothing of consequence has actually transpired, and the interactions of the characters do not bring enough back.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3983339197687934599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_352493193" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/352493193" />
    <a title="Vicki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4628162-vicki"><img alt="Vicki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291687024p2/4628162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/352493193?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vicki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vicki" href="/user/show/4628162-vicki">Vicki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer352493193" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11678847221812207067">Science fiction may not be for everybody, but IMHO, it ought to be! The science stretches your imagination with exciting technological possibilities and the fiction enriches your mind with great plotting and character development that equals or surpasses other mysteries and thrillers which don't happen to be set in remote outer space at some remote future time. So if you haven't been a sci fi fan, welcome aboard from here on!<br/><br/>In Earth Unaware, the authors have gone back to recapture the very begi</span>
  <span id="freeText11678847221812207067" style="display:none">Science fiction may not be for everybody, but IMHO, it ought to be! The science stretches your imagination with exciting technological possibilities and the fiction enriches your mind with great plotting and character development that equals or surpasses other mysteries and thrillers which don't happen to be set in remote outer space at some remote future time. So if you haven't been a sci fi fan, welcome aboard from here on!<br /><br />In Earth Unaware, the authors have gone back to recapture the very beginnings of the story told in the Ender's Game series of books and short stories that have captivated a wide audience of devoted readers for a couple of decades. We are introduced to a mining family that lives and works on El Cavador, a mining ship working far beyond Pluto in the Kuiper Belt. The ship is equipped to be a self-sufficient living and working space. Their purpose is to find asteroids rich in minerals and attach to them. Miners exit the ship with the equipment needed to excavate the minerals, smelt the metal into cylinders and send the cylinders back to Earth in quick ships. <br /> <br />But not every worker on the ship is a miner.Victor Delgado is the teen son of the ship's equipment maintenance worker. Victor has proved to be an innovative repairman, inventing new uses for old equipment, and has proved himself a definite asset to the family.<br /> <br />Since they are a space ship, there are family members who are monitoring surrounding space at all times for any asteroid, comet, or anything floating through space that might be a threat to their ship. One of the spotters, a fourteen year old girl who is an apprentice to her father, has seen something in deep space that doesn't make sense. It appears to be moving at a speed faster than light, which ought to be impossible, slows down and speeds up occasionally, and seems to be on a trajectory that could take it to earth. Should they be alarmed? Should they alert other ships of their discovery? Could it possibly be some kind of fluke? She hesitates to alert her father in case there is an explanation that proves her to be an ignorant alarmist, so she asks her friend Victor for advice. <br /><br />The two teens take the information to the ship's captain, a grandmother who is the very competent matriarch of the family, Concepcion. She calls a Council Meeting of the Elders, which is everyone on the ship over 40. Though there is disagreement over what to do, the Council settles on a plan to alert their closer space neighbors and keep watching.<br /><br />In the meantime, one of their space neighbors, a corporate mining ship, the Makarhu, larger and with infinitely more resources, has decided to bump El Cavador off the ore-rich asteroid they are mining so that the larger ship can test a newly invented mining device their researchers have developed. The ship is owned by the largest space-mining corporation in the solar system, Juke Limited, and is captained by the owner's son and heir apparent, Lem Jukes. Ruthlessness seems to be an inherited trait in the Jukes family.<br /><br />Perhaps the danger that El Cavador faces is much closer than the deep space threat that they perceive so far away. Will El Calvador even survive to deal with the ominous movement in deep space as the Makarhu deviously sneaks up on the older, smaller space vessel with the intent of cutting the cables attaching it to the asteroid? <br /> <br />Story lines are introduced on El Cavador, the Makarhu, and back on Earth, as military specialists develop an elite team of soldiers selected from the cream of nations all over the world. How will all the stories of all these characters finally come together?<br /> <br /> This is the type of story that the phrase, "page turner," was invented for! It's quite the saga...let's get started!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11678847221812207067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
